</div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">30-second sample from "<a href="/wiki/I_Have_a_Dream" title="I Have a Dream">I Have a Dream</a>" speech by Martin Luther King Jr. at the <a href="/wiki/March_on_Washington_for_Jobs_and_Freedom" title="March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom">March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom</a> on August 28, 1963</div>
</div>
<hr/></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The march did, however, make specific demands: an end to racial segregation in public schools; meaningful civil rights legislation, including a law prohibiting racial discrimination in employment; protection of civil rights workers from police brutality; a $2 <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a> for all workers; and self-government for <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, then governed by congressional committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Despite tensions, the march was a resounding success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Powers_1997_313_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Powers_1997_313-94">[94]</a></sup> More than a quarter of a million people of diverse ethnicities attended the event, sprawling from the steps of the Lincoln Memorial onto the <a href="/wiki/National_Mall" title="National Mall">National Mall</a> and around the reflecting pool. At the time, it was the largest gathering of protesters in Washington, D.C.'s history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Powers_1997_313_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Powers_1997_313-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>King delivered a 17-minute speech, later known as "<a href="/wiki/I_Have_a_Dream" title="I Have a Dream">I Have a Dream</a>". In the speech's most famous passage—in which he departed from his prepared text, possibly at the prompting of <a href="/wiki/Mahalia_Jackson" title="Mahalia Jackson">Mahalia Jackson</a>, who shouted behind him, "Tell them about the dream!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>—King said:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="poem">
<p>I say to you today, my friends, so even though we face the difficulties of today and tomorrow, I still have a dream. It is a dream deeply rooted in the American dream.<br/>
I have a dream that one day this nation will rise up and live out the true meaning of its creed: 'We hold these truths to be self-evident: that all men are created equal.'<br/>
I have a dream that one day on the red hills of Georgia the sons of former slaves and the sons of former slave owners will be able to sit down together at the table of brotherhood.<br/>
I have a dream that one day even the state of Mississippi, a state sweltering with the heat of injustice, sweltering with the heat of oppression, will be transformed into an oasis of freedom and justice.<br/>
I have a dream that my four little children will one day live in a nation where they will not be judged by the color of their skin but by the content of their character.<br/>
I have a dream today.<br/>
I have a dream that one day, down in Alabama, with its vicious racists, with its governor having his lips dripping with the words of interposition and nullification; one day right there in Alabama, little black boys and black girls will be able to join hands with little white boys and white girls as sisters and brothers.<br/>
I have a dream today.</p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p>"I Have a Dream" came to be regarded as one of the finest speeches in the history of American oratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The March, and especially King's speech, helped put civil rights at the top of the agenda of reformers in the United States and facilitated passage of the Civil Rights Act of 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>The original typewritten copy of the speech, including King's handwritten notes on it, was discovered in 1984 to be in the hands of <a href="/wiki/George_Raveling" title="George Raveling">George Raveling</a>, the first African-American basketball coach of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Iowa" title="University of Iowa">University of Iowa</a>. In 1963, Raveling, then 26, was standing near the podium, and immediately after the oration, impulsively asked King if he could have his copy of the speech. He got it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBS_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-CBS-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Selma_voting_rights_movement_and_.22Bloody_Sunday.22.2C_1965">Selma voting rights movement and "Bloody Sunday", 1965</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Selma_to_Montgomery_marches" title="Selma to Montgomery marches">Selma to Montgomery marches</a></div>
<p>Acting on James Bevel's call for a march from Selma to Montgomery, King, Bevel, and the SCLC, in partial collaboration with SNCC, attempted to organize the march to the state's capital. The first attempt to march on March 7, 1965, was aborted because of mob and police violence against the demonstrators. This day has become known as <a href="/wiki/Selma_to_Montgomery_marches" title="Selma to Montgomery marches">Bloody Sunday</a> and was a major turning point in the effort to gain public support for the Civil Rights Movement. It was the clearest demonstration up to that time of the dramatic potential of King's nonviolence strategy. King, however, was not present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing1998_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing1998-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Selma_to_Montgomery_Marches_protesters.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="611" data-file-width="899" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Selma_to_Montgomery_Marches_protesters.jpg/220px-Selma_to_Montgomery_Marches_protesters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Selma_to_Montgomery_Marches_protesters.jpg/330px-Selma_to_Montgomery_Marches_protesters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Selma_to_Montgomery_Marches_protesters.jpg/440px-Selma_to_Montgomery_Marches_protesters.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Selma_to_Montgomery_Marches_protesters.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The civil rights <a href="/wiki/Selma_to_Montgomery_marches" title="Selma to Montgomery marches">march from Selma to Montgomery</a>, Alabama, in 1965</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>King met with officials in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Administration" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Administration">Lyndon B. Johnson Administration</a> on March 5 in order to request an <a href="/wiki/Injunction" title="Injunction">injunction</a> against any prosecution of the demonstrators. He did not attend the march due to church duties, but he later wrote, "If I had any idea that the state troopers would use the kind of brutality they did, I would have felt compelled to give up my church duties altogether to lead the line."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing1998276.E2.80.9379_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing1998276.E2.80.9379-103">[103]</a></sup> Footage of <a href="/wiki/Police_brutality" title="Police brutality">police brutality</a> against the protesters was broadcast extensively and aroused national public outrage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2006222.E2.80.9323_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2006222.E2.80.9323-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>King next attempted to organize a march for March 9. The SCLC petitioned for an injunction in federal court against the State of Alabama; this was denied and the judge issued an order blocking the march until after a hearing. Nonetheless, King led marchers on March 9 to the <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Pettus_Bridge" title="Edmund Pettus Bridge">Edmund Pettus Bridge</a> in Selma, then held a short prayer session before turning the marchers around and asking them to disperse so as not to violate the court order. The unexpected ending of this second march aroused the surprise and anger of many within the local movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2006223_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2006223-105">[105]</a></sup> The march finally went ahead fully on March 25, 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> At the conclusion of the march on the steps of the <a href="/wiki/Alabama_State_Capitol" title="Alabama State Capitol">state capitol</a>, King delivered a speech that became known as "<a href="/wiki/How_Long,_Not_Long" title="How Long, Not Long">How Long, Not Long</a>". In it, King stated that equal rights for African Americans could not be far away, "because the arc of the moral universe is long, but it bends toward justice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chicago_open_housing_movement.2C_1966">Chicago open housing movement, 1966</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Freedom_Movement" title="Chicago Freedom Movement">Chicago Freedom Movement</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King,_Jr._and_Lyndon_Johnson_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2002" data-file-width="3000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Lyndon_Johnson_2.jpg/220px-Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Lyndon_Johnson_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Lyndon_Johnson_2.jpg/330px-Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Lyndon_Johnson_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Lyndon_Johnson_2.jpg/440px-Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Lyndon_Johnson_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King,_Jr._and_Lyndon_Johnson_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> meets with King in the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_Room_(White_House)" title="Cabinet Room (White House)">White House Cabinet Room</a>, 1966</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1966, after several successes in the south, King, Bevel, and others in the civil rights organizations tried to spread the movement to the North, with <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> as their first destination. King and Ralph Abernathy, both from the middle class, moved into a building at 1550 S. Hamlin Ave., in the slums of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Lawndale" title="North Lawndale">North Lawndale</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> on Chicago's West Side, as an educational experience and to demonstrate their support and empathy for the poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2000360.E2.80.9362_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2000360.E2.80.9362-112">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>The SCLC formed a coalition with CCCO, Coordinating Council of Community Organizations, an organization founded by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Raby" title="Albert Raby">Albert Raby</a>, and the combined organizations' efforts were fostered under the aegis of the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Freedom_Movement" title="Chicago Freedom Movement">Chicago Freedom Movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ralph_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ralph-113">[112]</a></sup> During that spring, several white couple / black couple tests of real estate offices uncovered <a href="/wiki/Racial_steering" title="Racial steering">racial steering</a>: discriminatory processing of housing requests by couples who were exact matches in income, background, number of children, and other attributes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2000347_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2000347-114">[113]</a></sup> Several larger marches were planned and executed: in Bogan, <a href="/wiki/Belmont_Cragin,_Chicago" title="Belmont Cragin, Chicago">Belmont Cragin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Park,_Chicago" title="Jefferson Park, Chicago">Jefferson Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evergreen_Park,_Illinois" title="Evergreen Park, Illinois">Evergreen Park</a> (a suburb southwest of Chicago), <a href="/wiki/Gage_Park,_Chicago" title="Gage Park, Chicago">Gage Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marquette_Park_(Chicago)" title="Marquette Park (Chicago)">Marquette Park</a>, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ralph_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ralph-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen2000416_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen2000416-115">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>Abernathy later wrote that the movement received a worse reception in Chicago than in the South. Marches, especially the one through Marquette Park on August 5, 1966, were met by thrown bottles and screaming throngs. Rioting seemed very possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> King's beliefs militated against his staging a violent event, and he negotiated an agreement with Mayor <a href="/wiki/Richard_J._Daley" title="Richard J. Daley">Richard J. Daley</a> to cancel a march in order to avoid the violence that he feared would result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> King was hit by a brick during one march but continued to lead marches in the face of personal danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>When King and his allies returned to the South, they left <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Jackson" title="Jesse Jackson">Jesse Jackson</a>, a seminary student who had previously joined the movement in the South, in charge of their organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> Jackson continued their struggle for civil rights by organizing the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Breadbasket" title="Operation Breadbasket">Operation Breadbasket</a> movement that targeted chain stores that did not deal fairly with blacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_to_the_Vietnam_War">Opposition to the Vietnam War</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opposition_to_the_U.S._involvement_in_the_Vietnam_War" title="Opposition to the U.S. involvement in the Vietnam War">Opposition to the U.S. involvement in the Vietnam War</a></div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em">
<caption>External audio</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> You may watch the speech, "Why I Am Opposed to the War in Vietnam", by Martin Luther King <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/MartinLutherKingJr.whyIAmOpposedToTheWarInVietnam" rel="nofollow"><b>here</b></a>.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>King long opposed American involvement in the Vietnam War,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingandvietnam1_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-kingandvietnam1-123">[122]</a></sup> but at first avoided the topic in public speeches in order to avoid the interference with civil rights goals that criticism of President Johnson's policies might have created.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingandvietnam1_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-kingandvietnam1-123">[122]</a></sup> However, at the urging of SCLC's former Director of Direct Action and now the head of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_Mobilization_Committee_to_End_the_War_in_Vietnam" title="Spring Mobilization Committee to End the War in Vietnam">Spring Mobilization Committee to End the War in Vietnam</a>, James Bevel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingandvietnam2_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-kingandvietnam2-124">[123]</a></sup> King eventually agreed to publicly oppose the war as opposition was growing among the American public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingandvietnam1_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-kingandvietnam1-123">[122]</a></sup> In an April 4, 1967, appearance at the New York City <a href="/wiki/Riverside_Church" title="Riverside Church">Riverside Church</a>—exactly one year before his death—King delivered a speech titled "<a href="/wiki/Beyond_Vietnam:_A_Time_to_Break_Silence" title="Beyond Vietnam: A Time to Break Silence">Beyond Vietnam: A Time to Break Silence</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vwar29_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-vwar29-125">[124]</a></sup> He spoke strongly against the U.S.'s role in the war, arguing that the U.S. was in Vietnam "to occupy it as an American colony"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobbins2007107_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobbins2007107-126">[125]</a></sup> and calling the U.S. government "the greatest purveyor of violence in the world today".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobbins2007102_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobbins2007102-127">[126]</a></sup> He also connected the war with economic injustice, arguing that the country needed serious moral change:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>A true revolution of values will soon look uneasily on the glaring contrast of poverty and wealth. With righteous indignation, it will look across the seas and see individual capitalists of the West investing huge sums of money in Asia, Africa and South America, only to take the profits out with no concern for the social betterment of the countries, and say: "This is not just."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobbins2007109_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobbins2007109-128">[127]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>King also opposed the Vietnam War because it took money and resources that could have been spent on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_welfare" title="Social welfare">social welfare</a> at home. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> was spending more and more on the military and less and less on <a href="/wiki/Poverty_reduction" title="Poverty reduction">anti-poverty programs</a> at the same time. He summed up this aspect by saying, "A nation that continues year after year to spend more money on military defense than on programs of social uplift is approaching spiritual death".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobbins2007109_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobbins2007109-128">[127]</a></sup> He stated that North Vietnam "did not begin to send in any large number of supplies or men until American forces had arrived in the tens of thousands",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobbins2007106_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobbins2007106-129">[128]</a></sup> and accused the U.S. of having killed a million Vietnamese, "mostly children".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> King also criticized American opposition to North Vietnam's land reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>King's opposition cost him significant support among white allies, including President Johnson, Billy Graham,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> union leaders and powerful publishers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MED08_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-MED08-133">[132]</a></sup> "The press is being stacked against me", King said,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup> complaining of what he described as a double standard that applauded his nonviolence at home, but deplored it when applied "toward little brown Vietnamese children".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pierre2011_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pierre2011-135">[134]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i> magazine called the speech "demagogic slander that sounded like a script for <a href="/wiki/Radio_Hanoi" title="Radio Hanoi">Radio Hanoi</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobbins2007109_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobbins2007109-128">[127]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> declared that King had "diminished his usefulness to his cause, his country, his people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pierre2011_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pierre2011-135">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawson2006148_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawson2006148-136">[135]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_Jr_St_Paul_Campus_U_MN.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="439" data-file-width="640" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_St_Paul_Campus_U_MN.jpg/220px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_St_Paul_Campus_U_MN.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_St_Paul_Campus_U_MN.jpg/330px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_St_Paul_Campus_U_MN.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_St_Paul_Campus_U_MN.jpg/440px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_St_Paul_Campus_U_MN.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_Jr_St_Paul_Campus_U_MN.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
King speaking to an anti-Vietnam war rally at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Minnesota" title="University of Minnesota">University of Minnesota</a> in St. Paul, April 27, 1967</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The "Beyond Vietnam" speech reflected King's evolving political advocacy in his later years, which paralleled the teachings of the progressive <a href="/wiki/Highlander_Research_and_Education_Center" title="Highlander Research and Education Center">Highlander Research and Education Center</a>, with which he was affiliated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> King began to speak of the need for fundamental changes in the political and economic life of the nation, and more frequently expressed his opposition to the war and his desire to see a redistribution of resources to correct racial and economic injustice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> He guarded his language in public to avoid being linked to <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a> by his enemies, but in private he sometimes spoke of his support for <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">democratic socialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup> In a 1952 letter to Coretta Scott, he said: "I imagine you already know that I am much more socialistic in my economic theory than capitalistic ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> In one speech, he stated that "something is wrong with capitalism" and claimed, "There must be a better distribution of wealth, and maybe America must move toward a democratic socialism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup> King had read <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Marx</a> while at Morehouse, but while he rejected "traditional capitalism", he also rejected communism because of its "materialistic interpretation of history" that denied religion, its "ethical relativism", and its "political totalitarianism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>King also stated in "Beyond Vietnam" that "true compassion is more than flinging a coin to a beggar ... it comes to see that an edifice which produces beggars needs restructuring".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_2002_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn_2002-145">[144]</a></sup> King quoted a United States official who said that from Vietnam to Latin America, the country was "on the wrong side of a world revolution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_2002_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn_2002-145">[144]</a></sup> King condemned America's "alliance with the landed gentry of Latin America", and said that the U.S. should support "the shirtless and barefoot people" in the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a> rather than suppressing their attempts at revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_2002_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn_2002-145">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 15, 1967, King participated in and spoke at an anti-war march from New York's Central Park to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> organized by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_Mobilization_Committee_to_End_the_War_in_Vietnam" title="Spring Mobilization Committee to End the War in Vietnam">Spring Mobilization Committee to End the War in Vietnam</a> and initiated by its chairman, James Bevel. At the U.N. King also brought up issues of civil rights and the draft.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I have not urged a mechanical fusion of the civil rights and peace movements. There are people who have come to see the moral imperative of equality, but who cannot yet see the moral imperative of world brotherhood. I would like to see the fervor of the civil-rights movement imbued into the peace movement to instill it with greater strength. And I believe everyone has a duty to be in both the civil-rights and peace movements. But for those who presently choose but one, I would hope they will finally come to see the moral roots common to both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Seeing an opportunity to unite civil rights activists and anti-war activists,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingandvietnam2_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-kingandvietnam2-124">[123]</a></sup> Bevel convinced King to become even more active in the anti-war effort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingandvietnam2_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-kingandvietnam2-124">[123]</a></sup> Despite his growing public opposition towards the Vietnam War, King was also not fond of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counterculture_movement" title="Counterculture movement">hippie culture</a> which developed from the anti-war movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingandvietnam3_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-kingandvietnam3-147">[146]</a></sup> In his 1967 <a href="/wiki/Massey_Lectures" title="Massey Lectures">Massey Lecture</a>, King stated:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The importance of the hippies is not in their unconventional behavior, but in the fact that hundreds of thousands of young people, in turning to a flight from reality, are expressing a profoundly discrediting view on the society they emerge from.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingandvietnam3_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-kingandvietnam3-147">[146]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>On January 13, 1968, the day after President Johnson's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_Address" title="State of the Union Address">State of the Union Address</a>, King called for a large march on Washington against "one of history's most cruel and senseless wars".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kurlansky2004_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-kurlansky2004-148">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt-13jan1968_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-13jan1968-149">[148]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We need to make clear in this political year, to congressmen on both sides of the aisle and to the president of the United States, that we will no longer tolerate, we will no longer vote for men who continue to see the killings of Vietnamese and Americans as the best way of advancing the goals of freedom and self-determination in Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kurlansky2004_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-kurlansky2004-148">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt-13jan1968_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-13jan1968-149">[148]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Poor_People.27s_Campaign.2C_1968">Poor People's Campaign, 1968</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Poor_People%27s_Campaign" title="Poor People's Campaign">Poor People's Campaign</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Resurrection_City_Washington_D.C._1968.jpg"><img alt="Rows of tents" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="758" data-file-width="1136" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Resurrection_City_Washington_D.C._1968.jpg/220px-Resurrection_City_Washington_D.C._1968.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Resurrection_City_Washington_D.C._1968.jpg/330px-Resurrection_City_Washington_D.C._1968.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Resurrection_City_Washington_D.C._1968.jpg/440px-Resurrection_City_Washington_D.C._1968.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Resurrection_City_Washington_D.C._1968.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A shantytown was established in Washington, D. C. to protest economic conditions as a part of the Poor People's Campaign</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1968, King and the SCLC organized the "<a href="/wiki/Poor_People%27s_Campaign" title="Poor People's Campaign">Poor People's Campaign</a>" to address issues of economic justice. King traveled the country to assemble "a multiracial army of the poor" that would march on Washington to engage in nonviolent <a href="/wiki/Civil_disobedience" title="Civil disobedience">civil disobedience</a> at the Capitol until Congress created an "economic bill of rights" for poor Americans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lied_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-lied-151">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>The campaign was preceded by King's final book, <i><a href="/wiki/Where_Do_We_Go_from_Here:_Chaos_or_Community%3F" title="Where Do We Go from Here: Chaos or Community?">Where Do We Go from Here: Chaos or Community?</a></i> which laid out his view of how to address social issues and poverty. King quoted from <a href="/wiki/Henry_George" title="Henry George">Henry George</a> and George's book, <i><a href="/wiki/Progress_and_Poverty" title="Progress and Poverty">Progress and Poverty</a></i>, particularly in support of a <a href="/wiki/Basic_income" title="Basic income">guaranteed basic income</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup> The campaign culminated in a march on Washington, D.C., demanding economic aid to the poorest communities of the United States.</p>
<p>King and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Christian_Leadership_Conference" title="Southern Christian Leadership Conference">SCLC</a> called on the government to invest in rebuilding America's cities. He felt that Congress had shown "hostility to the poor" by spending "military funds with alacrity and generosity". He contrasted this with the situation faced by poor Americans, claiming that Congress had merely provided "poverty funds with miserliness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lied_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-lied-151">[150]</a></sup> His vision was for change that was more revolutionary than mere reform: he cited systematic flaws of "racism, poverty, militarism and materialism", and argued that "reconstruction of society itself is the real issue to be faced".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELawson2006148.E2.80.9349_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELawson2006148.E2.80.9349-155">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Poor People's Campaign was controversial even within the civil rights movement. Rustin resigned from the march, stating that the goals of the campaign were too broad, that its demands were unrealizable, and that he thought that these campaigns would accelerate the backlash and repression on the poor and the black.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_King.27s_death">After King's death</span></h3>
<p>The plan to set up a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shantytown" title="Shantytown">shantytown</a> in Washington, D.C., was carried out soon after the April 4 <a href="/wiki/Assassination" title="Assassination">assassination</a>. Criticism of King's plan was subdued in the wake of his death, and the SCLC received an unprecedented wave of donations for the purpose of carrying it out. The campaign officially began in Memphis, on May 2, at the hotel where King was murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKnight_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKnight-157">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thousands of demonstrators arrived on the <a href="/wiki/National_Mall" title="National Mall">National Mall</a> and established a camp they called "<a href="/wiki/Poor_People%27s_Campaign" title="Poor People's Campaign">Resurrection City</a>". They stayed for six weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engler15Jan10_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Engler15Jan10-158">[157]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassination_and_aftermath">Assassination and aftermath</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Assassination of Martin Luther King Jr.">Assassination of Martin Luther King Jr.</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_was_shot_here_Small_Web_view.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="239" data-file-width="320" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Martin_Luther_King_was_shot_here_Small_Web_view.jpg/220px-Martin_Luther_King_was_shot_here_Small_Web_view.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Martin_Luther_King_was_shot_here_Small_Web_view.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_was_shot_here_Small_Web_view.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Lorraine Motel, where King was assassinated, is now the site of the <a href="/wiki/National_Civil_Rights_Museum" title="National Civil Rights Museum">National Civil Rights Museum</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>

</div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">Final 30 seconds of "<a href="/wiki/I%27ve_Been_to_the_Mountaintop" title="I've Been to the Mountaintop">I've Been to the Mountaintop</a>" speech by Martin Luther King Jr.</div>
</div>
<hr/></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On March 29, 1968, King went to Memphis, Tennessee, in support of the black sanitary public works employees, who were represented by <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_State,_County_and_Municipal_Employees" title="American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees">AFSCME</a> Local 1733. The workers had been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memphis_Sanitation_Strike" title="Memphis Sanitation Strike">on strike</a> since March 12 for higher wages and better treatment. In one incident, black street repairmen received pay for two hours when they were sent home because of bad weather, but white employees were paid for the full day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFSCME-WEB1_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-AFSCME-WEB1-159">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFSCME-WEB2_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-AFSCME-WEB2-160">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 3, King addressed a rally and delivered his "I've Been to the Mountaintop" address at <a href="/wiki/Mason_Temple" title="Mason Temple">Mason Temple</a>, the world headquarters of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_God_in_Christ" title="Church of God in Christ">Church of God in Christ</a>. King's flight to Memphis had been delayed by a bomb threat against his plane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup> In the close of the last speech of his career, in reference to the bomb threat, King said the following:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>And then I got to Memphis. And some began to say the threats, or talk about the threats that were out. What would happen to me from some of our sick white brothers?</p>
<p>Well, I don't know what will happen now. We've got some difficult days ahead. But it doesn't matter with me now. Because I've been to the mountaintop. And I don't mind. Like anybody, I would like to live a long life. Longevity has its place. But I'm not concerned about that now. I just want to do God's will. And He's allowed me to go up to the mountain. And I've looked over. And I've seen the promised land. I may not get there with you. But I want you to know tonight, that we, as a people, will get to the promised land. So I'm happy, tonight. I'm not worried about anything. I'm not fearing any man. Mine eyes have seen the glory of the coming of the Lord.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>King was booked in Room 306 at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorraine_Motel" title="Lorraine Motel">Lorraine Motel</a> (owned by Walter Bailey) in Memphis. Abernathy, who was present at the assassination, testified to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Select_Committee_on_Assassinations" title="United States House Select Committee on Assassinations">United States House Select Committee on Assassinations</a> that King and his entourage stayed at Room 306 so often that it was known as the "King-Abernathy suite".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usdoj_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-usdoj-164">[163]</a></sup> According to Jesse Jackson, who was present, King's last words on the balcony before his assassination were spoken to musician <a href="/wiki/Ben_Branch" title="Ben Branch">Ben Branch</a>, who was scheduled to perform that night at an event King was attending: "Ben, make sure you play '<a href="/wiki/Take_My_Hand,_Precious_Lord" title="Take My Hand, Precious Lord">Take My Hand, Precious Lord</a>' in the meeting tonight. Play it real pretty."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>King was shot by <a href="/wiki/James_Earl_Ray" title="James Earl Ray">James Earl Ray</a> at 6:01 p.m., April 4, 1968, as he stood on the motel's second-floor balcony. The bullet entered through his right cheek, smashing his jaw, then traveled down his spinal cord before lodging in his shoulder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CHI_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-CHI-166">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup> Abernathy heard the shot from inside the motel room and ran to the balcony to find King on the floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrady2005204.E2.80.9305_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrady2005204.E2.80.9305-168">[167]</a></sup> Jackson stated after the shooting that he cradled King's head as King lay on the balcony, but this account was disputed by other colleagues of King; Jackson later changed his statement to say that he had "reached out" for King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>After emergency chest surgery, King died at <a href="/wiki/St._Joseph%27s_Hospital_(Memphis,_Tennessee)" title="St. Joseph's Hospital (Memphis, Tennessee)">St. Joseph's Hospital</a> at 7:05 p.m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup> According to biographer <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Branch" title="Taylor Branch">Taylor Branch</a>, King's autopsy revealed that though only 39 years old, he "had the heart of a 60 year old", which Branch attributed to the stress of 13 years in the Civil Rights Movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/King_assassination_riots" title="King assassination riots">King assassination riots</a></div>
<p>The assassination led to a nationwide wave of <a href="/wiki/Mass_racial_violence_in_the_United_States" title="Mass racial violence in the United States">race riots</a> in <a href="/wiki/1968_Washington,_D.C._riots" title="1968 Washington, D.C. riots">Washington, D.C.</a>, <a href="/wiki/1968_Chicago_riots" title="1968 Chicago riots">Chicago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_riot_of_1968" title="Baltimore riot of 1968">Baltimore</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louisville_riots_of_1968" title="Louisville riots of 1968">Louisville</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1968_Kansas_City_riot" title="1968 Kansas City riot">Kansas City</a>, and dozens of other cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-172">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup> Presidential candidate Robert F. "Bobby" Kennedy was on his way to <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis" title="Indianapolis">Indianapolis</a> for a campaign rally when he was informed of King's death. He gave <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy%27s_speech_on_the_assassination_of_Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Robert F. Kennedy's speech on the assassination of Martin Luther King Jr.">a short speech</a> to the gathering of supporters informing them of the tragedy and urging them to continue King's ideal of nonviolence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Farmer_Jr." title="James Farmer Jr.">James Farmer Jr.</a>, and other civil rights leaders also called for non-violent action, while the more militant Stokely Carmichael called for a more forceful response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1968_Year_In_Review.2C_UPI.com_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-1968_Year_In_Review.2C_UPI.com-175">[174]</a></sup> The city of Memphis quickly settled the strike on terms favorable to the sanitation workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFSCME-WEB3_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-AFSCME-WEB3-176">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>President Lyndon B. Johnson declared April 7 a national day of mourning for the civil rights leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> Vice President <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Humphrey" title="Hubert Humphrey">Hubert Humphrey</a> attended King's funeral on behalf of the President, as there were fears that Johnson's presence might incite protests and perhaps violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup> At his widow's request, King's last sermon at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Baptist_Church_(Atlanta,_Georgia)" title="Ebenezer Baptist Church (Atlanta, Georgia)">Ebenezer Baptist Church</a> was played at the funeral,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup> a recording of his "Drum Major" sermon, given on February 4, 1968. In that sermon, King made a request that at his funeral no mention of his awards and honors be made, but that it be said that he tried to "feed the hungry", "clothe the naked", "be right on the [Vietnam] war question", and "love and serve humanity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MahaliaJackson1964.jpg"><img alt="Jackson standing onstage in a long white dress" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2339" data-file-width="2125" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/MahaliaJackson1964.jpg/220px-MahaliaJackson1964.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/MahaliaJackson1964.jpg/330px-MahaliaJackson1964.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/MahaliaJackson1964.jpg/440px-MahaliaJackson1964.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MahaliaJackson1964.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
King's friend <a href="/wiki/Mahalia_Jackson" title="Mahalia Jackson">Mahalia Jackson</a> (see here in 1964) sang at his funeral</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>His good friend Mahalia Jackson sang his favorite hymn, "Take My Hand, Precious Lord", at the funeral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two months after King's death, escaped convict <a href="/wiki/James_Earl_Ray" title="James Earl Ray">James Earl Ray</a> was captured at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_Heathrow_Airport" title="London Heathrow Airport">London Heathrow Airport</a> while trying to leave the United Kingdom on a false Canadian passport in the name of Ramon George Sneyd on his way to white-ruled <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup> Ray was quickly extradited to Tennessee and charged with King's murder. He confessed to the assassination on March 10, 1969, though he recanted this confession three days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extradite_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-extradite-183">[182]</a></sup> On the advice of his attorney <a href="/wiki/Percy_Foreman" title="Percy Foreman">Percy Foreman</a>, Ray pleaded guilty to avoid a trial conviction and thus the possibility of receiving the death penalty. He was sentenced to a 99-year prison term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extradite_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-extradite-183">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbs_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-cbs-184">[183]</a></sup> Ray later claimed a man he met in <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>, Quebec, with the alias "Raoul" was involved and that the assassination was the result of a conspiracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis-186">[185]</a></sup> He spent the remainder of his life attempting, unsuccessfully, to withdraw his guilty plea and secure the trial he never had.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbs_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-cbs-184">[183]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allegations_of_conspiracy">Allegations of conspiracy</span></h3>
<p>Ray's lawyers maintained he was a <a href="/wiki/Scapegoat" title="Scapegoat">scapegoat</a> similar to the way that John F. Kennedy assassin <a href="/wiki/Lee_Harvey_Oswald" title="Lee Harvey Oswald">Lee Harvey Oswald</a> is seen by conspiracy theorists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN1_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNN1-187">[186]</a></sup> Supporters of this assertion said that Ray's confession was given under pressure and that he had been threatened with the death penalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbs_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-cbs-184">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup> They admitted that Ray was a thief and burglar, but claimed that he had no record of committing violent crimes with a weapon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-davis-186">[185]</a></sup> However, prison records in different U.S. cities have shown that he was incarcerated on numerous occasions for charges of armed robbery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mlkassassin_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-mlkassassin-189">[188]</a></sup> In a 2008 interview with CNN, Jerry Ray, the younger brother of James Earl Ray, claimed that James was smart and was sometimes able to get away with armed robbery. Jerry Ray said that he had assisted his brother on one such robbery. "I never been with nobody as bold as he is," Jerry said. "He just walked in and put that gun on somebody, it was just like it's an everyday thing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mlkassassin_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-mlkassassin-189">[188]</a></sup></p>
<p>Those suspecting a conspiracy in the assassination point to the two successive <a href="/wiki/Ballistics" title="Ballistics">ballistics</a> tests which proved that a rifle similar to Ray's <a href="/wiki/Remington_Arms" title="Remington Arms">Remington</a> Gamemaster had been the murder weapon. Those tests did not implicate Ray's specific rifle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbs_184-3"><a href="#cite_note-cbs-184">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-WEB1_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-WEB1-190">[189]</a></sup> Witnesses near King at the moment of his death said that the shot came from another location. They said that it came from behind thick shrubbery near the boarding house—which had been cut away in the days following the assassination—and not from the boarding house window.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerold_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerold-191">[190]</a></sup> However, Ray's fingerprints were found on various objects (a rifle, a pair of binoculars, articles of clothing, a newspaper) that were left in the bathroom where it was determined the gunfire came from.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mlkassassin_189-2"><a href="#cite_note-mlkassassin-189">[188]</a></sup> An examination of the rifle containing Ray's fingerprints also determined that at least one shot was fired from the firearm at the time of the assassination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mlkassassin_189-3"><a href="#cite_note-mlkassassin-189">[188]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_Jr_Coretta_Scott_King_Tomb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_Coretta_Scott_King_Tomb.jpg/220px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_Coretta_Scott_King_Tomb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_Coretta_Scott_King_Tomb.jpg/330px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_Coretta_Scott_King_Tomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_Coretta_Scott_King_Tomb.jpg/440px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_Coretta_Scott_King_Tomb.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_Jr_Coretta_Scott_King_Tomb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Martin Luther King and Coretta Scott King's <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a>, located on the grounds of the <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr._National_Historic_Site" title="Martin Luther King Jr. National Historic Site">Martin Luther King Jr. National Historic Site</a> in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta, Georgia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1997, King's son Dexter Scott King met with Ray, and publicly supported Ray's efforts to obtain a <a href="/wiki/New_trial" title="New trial">new trial</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN2_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNN2-192">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two years later, Coretta Scott King, King's widow, along with the rest of King's family, won a <a href="/wiki/Wrongful_death_claim" title="Wrongful death claim">wrongful death claim</a> against <a href="/wiki/Loyd_Jowers" title="Loyd Jowers">Loyd Jowers</a> and "other unknown co-conspirators". Jowers claimed to have received $100,000 to arrange King's assassination. The jury of six whites and six blacks found in favor of the King family, finding Jowers to be complicit in a conspiracy against King and that government agencies were party to the assassination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_F._Pepper" title="William F. Pepper">William F. Pepper</a> represented the King family in the trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2000, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Justice" title="U.S. Department of Justice">U.S. Department of Justice</a> completed the investigation into Jowers' claims but did not find evidence to support allegations about conspiracy. The investigation report recommended no further investigation unless some new reliable facts are presented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usdoj2_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-usdoj2-196">[195]</a></sup> A sister of Jowers admitted that he had fabricated the story so he could make $300,000 from selling the story, and she in turn corroborated his story in order to get some money to pay her income tax.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2002, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> reported that a church minister, Rev. Ronald Denton Wilson, claimed his father, Henry Clay Wilson—not James Earl Ray—assassinated King. He stated, "It wasn't a racist thing; he thought Martin Luther King was connected with communism, and he wanted to get him out of the way." Wilson provided no evidence to back up his claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTORIG_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTORIG-199">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>King researchers <a href="/wiki/David_Garrow" title="David Garrow">David Garrow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Posner" title="Gerald Posner">Gerald Posner</a> disagreed with William F. Pepper's claims that the government killed King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[199]</a></sup> In 2003, Pepper published a book about the long investigation and trial, as well as his representation of James Earl Ray in his bid for a trial, laying out the evidence and criticizing other accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup> King's friend and colleague, James Bevel, also disputed the argument that Ray acted alone, stating, "There is no way a ten-cent white boy could develop a plan to kill a million-dollar black man."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup> In 2004, Jesse Jackson stated:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The fact is there were saboteurs to disrupt the march. And within our own organization, we found a very key person who was on the government payroll. So infiltration within, saboteurs from without and the press attacks. ... I will never believe that James Earl Ray had the motive, the money and the mobility to have done it himself. Our government was very involved in setting the stage for and I think the escape route for James Earl Ray.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demo_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Demo-203">[202]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act,_July_2,_1964.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4687" data-file-width="7000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act%2C_July_2%2C_1964.jpg/220px-Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act%2C_July_2%2C_1964.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act%2C_July_2%2C_1964.jpg/330px-Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act%2C_July_2%2C_1964.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act%2C_July_2%2C_1964.jpg/440px-Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act%2C_July_2%2C_1964.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
