President Johnson signs the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a>. Among the guests behind him is Martin Luther King.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_memorial_Westminster_Abbey.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1074" data-file-width="466" height="392" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Martin_Luther_King_memorial_Westminster_Abbey.jpg/170px-Martin_Luther_King_memorial_Westminster_Abbey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Martin_Luther_King_memorial_Westminster_Abbey.jpg/255px-Martin_Luther_King_memorial_Westminster_Abbey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Martin_Luther_King_memorial_Westminster_Abbey.jpg/340px-Martin_Luther_King_memorial_Westminster_Abbey.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_memorial_Westminster_Abbey.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Martin Luther King Jr. statue over the west entrance of <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, installed in 1998</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%27Today_capitalism_has_outlived_its_usefulness%27_MLK.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/%27Today_capitalism_has_outlived_its_usefulness%27_MLK.jpg/220px-%27Today_capitalism_has_outlived_its_usefulness%27_MLK.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/%27Today_capitalism_has_outlived_its_usefulness%27_MLK.jpg/330px-%27Today_capitalism_has_outlived_its_usefulness%27_MLK.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/%27Today_capitalism_has_outlived_its_usefulness%27_MLK.jpg/440px-%27Today_capitalism_has_outlived_its_usefulness%27_MLK.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%27Today_capitalism_has_outlived_its_usefulness%27_MLK.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Protesters at the <a href="/wiki/2012_Republican_National_Convention" title="2012 Republican National Convention">2012 Republican National Convention</a> display King's words and image on a banner</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>King's main legacy was to secure progress on civil rights in the U.S. Just days after King's assassination, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1968" title="Civil Rights Act of 1968">Civil Rights Act of 1968</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HUDHistory_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-HUDHistory-204">[203]</a></sup> Title VIII of the Act, commonly known as the Fair Housing Act, prohibited discrimination in housing and housing-related transactions on the basis of race, religion, or national origin (later expanded to include sex, familial status, and disability). This legislation was seen as a tribute to King's struggle in his final years to combat residential discrimination in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HUDHistory_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-HUDHistory-204">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p>Internationally, King's legacy includes influences on the <a href="/wiki/Black_Consciousness_Movement" title="Black Consciousness Movement">Black Consciousness Movement</a> and Civil Rights Movement in South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup> King's work was cited by and served as an inspiration for South African leader <a href="/wiki/Albert_Lutuli" title="Albert Lutuli">Albert Lutuli</a>, who fought for racial justice in his country and was later awarded the Nobel Prize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing1992307.E2.80.9308_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing1992307.E2.80.9308-207">[206]</a></sup> The day following King's assassination, school teacher <a href="/wiki/Jane_Elliott" title="Jane Elliott">Jane Elliott</a> conducted her first "Blue Eyes/Brown Eyes" exercise with her class of elementary school students in <a href="/wiki/Riceville,_Iowa" title="Riceville, Iowa">Riceville</a>, Iowa. Her purpose was to help them understand King's death as it related to racism, something they little understood as they lived in a predominantly white community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup> King has become a national icon in the history of <a href="/wiki/Modern_liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Modern liberalism in the United States">American liberalism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_progressivism" title="American progressivism">American progressivism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup> King also influenced Irish politician and activist <a href="/wiki/John_Hume" title="John Hume">John Hume</a>. Hume, the former leader of the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_and_Labour_Party" title="Social Democratic and Labour Party">Social Democratic and Labour Party</a>, cited King's legacy as quintessential to the Northern Irish civil rights movement and the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a>, calling him "one of my great heroes of the century."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>King's wife, Coretta Scott King, followed in her husband's footsteps and was active in matters of social justice and civil rights until her death in 2006. The same year that Martin Luther King was assassinated, she established the King Center in Atlanta, Georgia, dedicated to preserving his legacy and the work of championing nonviolent conflict resolution and tolerance worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC-WEB1_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-KC-WEB1-213">[212]</a></sup> Their son, Dexter King, serves as the center's chairman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup> Daughter Yolanda King, who died in 2007, was a motivational speaker, author and founder of Higher Ground Productions, an organization specializing in diversity training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[215]</a></sup></p>
<p>Even within the King family, members disagree about his religious and political views about gay, lesbian, bisexual and transgender people. King's widow Coretta said publicly that she believed her husband would have supported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gay_rights" title="Gay rights">gay rights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[216]</a></sup> However, his youngest child, Bernice King, has said publicly that he would have been opposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gay_marriage" title="Gay marriage">gay marriage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPR-WEB1_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPR-WEB1-218">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>On February 4, 1968, at the Ebenezer Baptist Church, in speaking about how he wished to be remembered after his death, King stated:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I'd like somebody to mention that day that Martin Luther King Jr. tried to give his life serving others. I'd like for somebody to say that day that Martin Luther King Jr. tried to love somebody.</p>
<p>I want you to say that day that I tried to be right on the war question. I want you to be able to say that day that I did try to feed the hungry. I want you to be able to say that day that I did try in my life to clothe those who were naked. I want you to say on that day that I did try in my life to visit those who were in prison. And I want you to say that I tried to love and serve humanity.</p>
<p>Yes, if you want to say that I was a drum major. Say that I was a drum major for justice. Say that I was a drum major for peace. I was a drum major for righteousness. And all of the other shallow things will not matter. I won't have any money to leave behind. I won't have the fine and luxurious things of life to leave behind. But I just want to leave a committed life behind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1968_Year_In_Review.2C_UPI.com_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-1968_Year_In_Review.2C_UPI.com-175">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[218]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Day">Martin Luther King Jr. Day</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Day" title="Martin Luther King Jr. Day">Martin Luther King Jr. Day</a></div>
<p>Beginning in 1971, cities such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis, Missouri</a>, and states established annual holidays to honor King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stlouis_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-stlouis-220">[219]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> Rose Garden on November 2, 1983, President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> signed a bill creating a federal holiday to honor King. Observed for the first time on January 20, 1986, it is called <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Day" title="Martin Luther King Jr. Day">Martin Luther King Jr. Day</a>. Following President <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>'s 1992 proclamation, the holiday is observed on the third Monday of January each year, near the time of King's birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[221]</a></sup> On January 17, 2000, for the first time, Martin Luther King Jr. Day was officially observed in all fifty U.S. states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[222]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> (1992), <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a> (1999) and <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> (2000) were the last three states to recognize the holiday. Utah previously celebrated the holiday at the same time but under the name Human Rights Day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[223]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liturgical_commemorations">Liturgical commemorations</span></h3>
<p>King is remembered as a martyr by the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Episcopal_Church)" title="Calendar of saints (Episcopal Church)">Episcopal Church in the United States of America</a> with an annual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feast_day" title="Feast day">feast day</a> on the anniversary of his death, April 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[224]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calendar_of_Saints_(Lutheran)" title="Calendar of Saints (Lutheran)">Evangelical Lutheran Church in America</a> commemorates King liturgically on the anniversary of his birth, January 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[225]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="UK_legacy_and_The_Martin_Luther_King_Peace_Committee">UK legacy and The Martin Luther King Peace Committee</span></h3>
<p>In the United Kingdom, The Northumbria and Newcastle Universities Martin Luther King Peace Committee<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[226]</a></sup> exists to honour King's legacy, as represented by his final visit to the UK to receive an honorary degree from Newcastle University in 1967.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[227]</a></sup> The Peace Committee operates out of the chaplaincies of the city's two universities, Northumbria and Newcastle, both of which remain centres for the study of Martin Luther King and the US Civil Rights Movement. Inspired by King's vision, it undertakes a range of activities across the UK as it seeks to "build cultures of peace."</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideas.2C_influences.2C_and_political_stances">Ideas, influences, and political stances</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<p>As a <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> minister, King's main influence was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> and the Christian gospels, which he would almost always quote in his religious meetings, speeches at church, and in public discourses. King's faith was strongly based in Jesus' commandment of <a href="/wiki/Golden_Rule" title="Golden Rule">loving your neighbor as yourself</a>, loving God above all, and loving your enemies, praying for them and blessing them. His <a href="/wiki/Nonviolence" title="Nonviolence">nonviolent</a> thought was also based in the injunction to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turn_the_other_cheek" title="Turn the other cheek">turn the other cheek</a></i> in the <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Sermon on the Mount</a>, and Jesus' teaching of putting the sword back into its place (Matthew 26:52).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[228]</a></sup> In his famous <a href="/wiki/Letter_from_Birmingham_Jail" title="Letter from Birmingham Jail">Letter from Birmingham Jail</a>, King urged action consistent with what he describes as Jesus' "extremist" love, and also quoted numerous other <a href="/wiki/Christian_pacifism" title="Christian pacifism">Christian pacifist</a> authors, which was very usual for him. In another sermon, he stated:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Before I was a civil rights leader, I was a preacher of the Gospel. This was my first calling and it still remains my greatest commitment. You know, actually all that I do in civil rights I do because I consider it a part of my ministry. I have no other ambitions in life but to achieve excellence in the Christian ministry. I don't plan to run for any political office. I don't plan to do anything but remain a preacher. And what I'm doing in this struggle, along with many others, grows out of my feeling that the preacher must be concerned about the whole man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[230]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In his speech "<a href="/wiki/I%27ve_Been_to_the_Mountaintop" title="I've Been to the Mountaintop">I've Been to the Mountaintop</a>", he stated that he just wanted to do <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/God%27s_will" title="God's will">God's will</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nonviolence">Nonviolence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington,_D.C._(Dr._Martin_Luther_King,_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.)_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1565" data-file-width="1974" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington%2C_D.C._%28Dr._Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.%29_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg/220px-Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington%2C_D.C._%28Dr._Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.%29_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington%2C_D.C._%28Dr._Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.%29_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg/330px-Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington%2C_D.C._%28Dr._Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.%29_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington%2C_D.C._%28Dr._Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.%29_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg/440px-Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington%2C_D.C._%28Dr._Martin_Luther_King%2C_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.%29_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Civil_Rights_March_on_Washington,_D.C._(Dr._Martin_Luther_King,_Jr._and_Mathew_Ahmann_in_a_crowd.)_-_NARA_-_542015_-_Restoration.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
King at the 1963 Civil Rights March on Washington, D.C.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg"><img alt="A close-up of Rustin" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1349" data-file-width="1146" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg/220px-BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg/330px-BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg/440px-BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BayardRustinAug1963-LibraryOfCongress_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
King worked alongside Quakers such as <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a> to develop non-violent tactics</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Veteran African-American civil rights activist <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Rustin" title="Bayard Rustin">Bayard Rustin</a> was King's first regular advisor on nonviolence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[231]</a></sup> King was also advised by the white activists <a href="/wiki/Harris_Wofford" title="Harris Wofford">Harris Wofford</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glenn_Smiley" title="Glenn Smiley">Glenn Smiley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mlk-kpp01.stanford.edu_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-mlk-kpp01.stanford.edu-233">[232]</a></sup> Rustin and Smiley came from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_pacifist" title="Christian pacifist">Christian pacifist</a> tradition, and Wofford and Rustin both studied Gandhi's teachings. Rustin had applied nonviolence with the <a href="/wiki/Journey_of_Reconciliation" title="Journey of Reconciliation">Journey of Reconciliation</a> campaign in the 1940s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[233]</a></sup> and Wofford had been promoting Gandhism to Southern blacks since the early 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mlk-kpp01.stanford.edu_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-mlk-kpp01.stanford.edu-233">[232]</a></sup> King had initially known little about <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Gandhi</a> and rarely used the term "nonviolence" during his early years of activism in the early 1950s. King initially believed in and practiced self-defense, even obtaining guns in his household as a means of defense against possible attackers. The pacifists guided King by showing him the alternative of nonviolent resistance, arguing that this would be a better means to accomplish his goals of civil rights than self-defense. King then vowed to no longer personally use arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[235]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the aftermath of the boycott, King wrote <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stride_Toward_Freedom" title="Stride Toward Freedom">Stride Toward Freedom</a></i>, which included the chapter <i><a class="external text" href="http://mlk-kpp01.stanford.edu/primarydocuments/Vol4/1-Sept-1958_MyPilgrimageToNonviolence.pdf" rel="nofollow">Pilgrimage to Nonviolence</a>.</i> King outlined his understanding of nonviolence, which seeks to win an opponent to friendship, rather than to humiliate or defeat him. The chapter draws from an address by Wofford, with Rustin and <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Levison" title="Stanley Levison">Stanley Levison</a> also providing guidance and ghostwriting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[236]</a></sup></p>
<p>Inspired by <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a>'s success with nonviolent activism, King had "for a long time ... wanted to take a trip to India".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[237]</a></sup> With assistance from Harris Wofford, the <a href="/wiki/American_Friends_Service_Committee" title="American Friends Service Committee">American Friends Service Committee</a>, and other supporters, he was able to fund the journey in April 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing199213_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing199213-240">[239]</a></sup> The trip to India affected King, deepening his understanding of <a href="/wiki/Nonviolent_resistance" title="Nonviolent resistance">nonviolent resistance</a> and his commitment to America's struggle for civil rights. In a radio address made during his final evening in India, King reflected, "Since being in India, I am more convinced than ever before that the method of nonviolent resistance is the most potent weapon available to oppressed people in their struggle for justice and human dignity".</p>
<p>Bayard Rustin's open homosexuality, support of <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">democratic socialism</a>, and his former ties to the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_USA" title="Communist Party USA">Communist Party USA</a> caused many white and African-American leaders to demand King distance himself from Rustin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[240]</a></sup> which King agreed to do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrady200242_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrady200242-242">[241]</a></sup> However, King agreed that Rustin should be one of the main organizers of the 1963 March on Washington.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>King's admiration of Gandhi's nonviolence did not diminish in later years. He went so far as to hold up his example when receiving the Nobel Peace Prize in 1964, hailing the "successful precedent" of using nonviolence "in a magnificent way by Mohandas K. Gandhi to challenge the might of the British Empire ... He struggled only with the weapons of truth, soul force, non-injury and courage."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gandhi seemed to have influenced him with certain moral principles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing1992135.E2.80.9336_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing1992135.E2.80.9336-245">[244]</a></sup> though Gandhi himself had been influenced by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Kingdom_of_God_Is_Within_You" title="The Kingdom of God Is Within You">The Kingdom of God Is Within You</a></i>, a nonviolent classic written by Christian anarchist <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a>. In turn, both Gandhi and Martin Luther King had read Tolstoy, and King, Gandhi and Tolstoy had been strongly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>' Sermon on the Mount. King quoted Tolstoy's <i><a href="/wiki/War_and_Peace" title="War and Peace">War and Peace</a></i> in 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[245]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another influence for King's nonviolent method was <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a>'s essay <i><a href="/wiki/Civil_Disobedience_(Thoreau)" title="Civil Disobedience (Thoreau)">On Civil Disobedience</a></i>, which King read in his student days. He was influenced by the idea of refusing to cooperate with an evil system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[246]</a></sup> He also was greatly influenced by the works of Protestant theologians <a href="/wiki/Reinhold_Niebuhr" title="Reinhold Niebuhr">Reinhold Niebuhr</a> and Paul Tillich,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[247]</a></sup> as well as Walter Rauschenbusch's <i>Christianity and the Social Crisis</i>. King also sometimes used the concept of "<a href="/wiki/Agape" title="Agape">agape</a>" (brotherly Christian love).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[248]</a></sup> However, after 1960, he ceased employing it in his writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[249]</a></sup></p>
<p>Even after renouncing his personal use of guns, King had a complex relationship with the phenomenon of self-defense in the movement. He publicly discouraged it as a widespread practice, but acknowledged that it was sometimes necessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[250]</a></sup> Throughout his career King was frequently protected by other civil rights activists who carried arms, such as <a href="/wiki/Colonel_Stone_Johnson" title="Colonel Stone Johnson">Colonel Stone Johnson</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[251]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Augustine_Movement" title="St. Augustine Movement">Robert Hayling</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Deacons_for_Defense_and_Justice" title="Deacons for Defense and Justice">Deacons for Defense and Justice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[253]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h3>
<p>As the leader of the SCLC, King maintained a policy of not publicly endorsing a U.S. political party or candidate: "I feel someone must remain in the position of non-alignment, so that he can look objectively at both parties and be the conscience of both—not the servant or master of either."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oates1993_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oates1993-255">[254]</a></sup> In a 1958 interview, he expressed his view that neither party was perfect, saying, "I don't think the Republican party is a party full of the almighty God nor is the Democratic party. They both have weaknesses ... And I'm not inextricably bound to either party."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King-Carson2000p364_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-King-Carson2000p364-256">[255]</a></sup> King did praise Democratic Senator <a href="/wiki/Paul_Douglas" title="Paul Douglas">Paul Douglas</a> of Illinois as being the "greatest of all senators" because of his fierce advocacy for civil rights causes over the years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[256]</a></sup></p>
<p>King critiqued both parties' performance on promoting racial equality:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Actually, the Negro has been betrayed by both the Republican and the Democratic party. The Democrats have betrayed him by capitulating to the whims and caprices of the Southern Dixiecrats. The Republicans have betrayed him by capitulating to the blatant hypocrisy of reactionary right wing northern Republicans. And this coalition of southern Dixiecrats and right wing reactionary northern Republicans defeats every bill and every move towards liberal legislation in the area of civil rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King-Carson2000p84_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-King-Carson2000p84-258">[257]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Although King never publicly supported a political party or candidate for president, in a letter to a civil rights supporter in October 1956 he said that he was undecided as to whether he would vote for <a href="/wiki/Adlai_Stevenson_II" title="Adlai Stevenson II">Adlai Stevenson</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a>, but that "In the past I always voted the Democratic ticket."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King-Carson1992p384_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-King-Carson1992p384-259">[258]</a></sup> In his autobiography, King says that in 1960 he privately voted for Democratic candidate <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>: "I felt that Kennedy would make the best president. I never came out with an endorsement. My father did, but I never made one." King adds that he likely would have made an exception to his non-endorsement policy for a second Kennedy term, saying "Had President Kennedy lived, I would probably have endorsed him in 1964."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King-Carson1998p187_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-King-Carson1998p187-260">[259]</a></sup> In 1964, King urged his supporters "and all people of goodwill" to vote against Republican Senator <a href="/wiki/Barry_Goldwater" title="Barry Goldwater">Barry Goldwater</a> for president, saying that his election "would be a tragedy, and certainly suicidal almost, for the nation and the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[260]</a></sup> King supported the ideals of democratic socialism, although he was reluctant to speak directly of this support due to the anti-communist sentiment being projected throughout the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> at the time, and the association of socialism with communism. King believed that capitalism could not adequately provide the basic necessities of many American people, particularly the African-American community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[261]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compensation">Compensation</span></h3>
<p>King stated that black Americans, as well as other disadvantaged Americans, should be compensated for historical wrongs. In an interview conducted for <i><a href="/wiki/Playboy" title="Playboy">Playboy</a></i> in 1965, he said that granting black Americans only equality could not realistically close the economic gap between them and whites. King said that he did not seek a full restitution of wages lost to slavery, which he believed impossible, but proposed a government compensatory program of $50 billion over ten years to all disadvantaged groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWashington1991_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWashington1991-263">[262]</a></sup></p>
<p>He posited that "the money spent would be more than amply justified by the benefits that would accrue to the nation through a spectacular decline in school dropouts, family breakups, crime rates, illegitimacy, swollen relief rolls, rioting and other social evils".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWashington1991365.E2.80.9367_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWashington1991365.E2.80.9367-264">[263]</a></sup> He presented this idea as an application of the <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> regarding settlement of unpaid labor, but clarified that he felt that the money should not be spent exclusively on blacks. He stated, "It should benefit the disadvantaged of <i>all</i> races".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWashington1991367.E2.80.9368_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWashington1991367.E2.80.9368-265">[264]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_planning">Family planning</span></h3>
<p>On being awarded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planned_Parenthood_Federation_of_America" title="Planned Parenthood Federation of America">Planned Parenthood Federation of America</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Sanger_Awards" title="Margaret Sanger Awards">Margaret Sanger Award</a> on <a href="/wiki/Sermons_and_speeches_of_Martin_Luther_King_Jr.#cite_note-8" title="Sermons and speeches of Martin Luther King Jr.">5th May, 1966</a>, King said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Recently, the press has been filled with reports of sightings of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flying_saucers" title="Flying saucers">flying saucers</a>. While we need not give credence to these stories, they allow our imagination to speculate on how visitors from outer space would judge us. I am afraid they would be stupefied at our conduct. They would observe that for death planning we spend billions to create <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_cannon" title="Siege cannon">engines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military_tactics" title="Military tactics">strategies</a> for war. They would also observe that we spend millions to prevent death by <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a> and other causes. Finally they would observe that we spend paltry sums for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_planning" title="Population planning">population planning</a>, even though its <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">spontaneous growth</a> is an urgent threat to life on our planet. Our visitors from outer space could be forgiven if they reported home that our planet is inhabited by a race of insane men whose future is bleak and uncertain.<br/>
There is no human circumstance more tragic than the persisting existence of a harmful condition for which a remedy is readily available. <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">Family planning</a>, to relate population to <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">world resources</a>, is possible, practical and necessary. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)#First_Pandemic:_Early_Middle_Ages_.28Plague_of_Justinian.29" title="Plague (disease)">plagues of the</a> <a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">dark ages</a> or contemporary diseases we do not yet understand, the modern plague of <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">overpopulation</a> is soluble by means we have discovered and with resources we possess.<br/>
What is lacking is not sufficient knowledge of the solution but universal consciousness of the gravity of the problem and education of the billions who are its victims...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[266]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="FBI_and_King.27s_personal_life">FBI and King's personal life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FBI_PPC_1.pdf"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="912" data-file-width="716" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/FBI_PPC_1.pdf/page1-220px-FBI_PPC_1.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/FBI_PPC_1.pdf/page1-330px-FBI_PPC_1.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/FBI_PPC_1.pdf/page1-440px-FBI_PPC_1.pdf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FBI_PPC_1.pdf" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An internal memo from the FBI attempting to disrupt the Poor People's Campaign with fraudulent claims about King—it was part of the larger <a href="/wiki/COINTELPRO" title="COINTELPRO">COINTELPRO</a> campaign against the anti-war and civil rights movements</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="FBI_surveillance_and_wiretapping">FBI surveillance and wiretapping</span></h3>
<p>FBI director <a href="/wiki/J._Edgar_Hoover" title="J. Edgar Hoover">J. Edgar Hoover</a> personally ordered surveillance of King, with the intent to undermine his power as a civil rights leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MED08_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-MED08-133">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Honey2007ch4_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-Honey2007ch4-268">[267]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Church_Committee" title="Church Committee">Church Committee</a>, a 1975 investigation by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Congress" title="U.S. Congress">U.S. Congress</a>, "From December 1963 until his death in 1968, Martin Luther King Jr. was the target of an intensive campaign by the Federal Bureau of Investigation to 'neutralize' him as an effective civil rights leader."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Church_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-Church-269">[268]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Bureau received authorization to proceed with wiretapping from Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Robert F. Kennedy">Robert F. Kennedy</a> in the fall of 1963<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the_atlantic_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-the_atlantic-270">[269]</a></sup> and informed President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, both of whom unsuccessfully tried to persuade King to dissociate himself from <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Levison" title="Stanley Levison">Stanley Levison</a>, a New York lawyer who had been involved with Communist Party USA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-right_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-right-271">[270]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKotz2005_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKotz2005-272">[271]</a></sup> Although Robert Kennedy only gave written approval for limited wiretapping of King's phones "on a trial basis, for a month or so",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerst2007372_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerst2007372-273">[272]</a></sup> Hoover extended the clearance so his men were "unshackled" to look for evidence in any areas of King's life they deemed worthy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerst2007372.E2.80.9374_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerst2007372.E2.80.9374-274">[273]</a></sup> The Bureau placed wiretaps on Levison's and King's home and office phones, and bugged King's rooms in hotels as he traveled across the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-right_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-right-271">[270]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-track_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-track-275">[274]</a></sup> In 1967, Hoover listed the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Christian_Leadership_Conference" title="Southern Christian Leadership Conference">SCLC</a> as a black nationalist hate group, with the instructions: "No opportunity should be missed to exploit through counterintelligence techniques the organizational and personal conflicts of the leaderships of the groups ... to insure the targeted group is disrupted, ridiculed, or discredited."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Honey2007ch4_268-1"><a href="#cite_note-Honey2007ch4-268">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[275]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NSA_monitoring_of_King.27s_communications">NSA monitoring of King's communications</span></h3>
<p>In a secret operation code-named "<a href="/wiki/Project_MINARET" title="Project MINARET">Minaret</a>", the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> (NSA) monitored the communications of leading Americans, including King, who criticized the U.S. war in Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theguardian.com_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian.com-277">[276]</a></sup> A review by the NSA itself concluded that Minaret was "disreputable if not outright illegal."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theguardian.com_277-1"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian.com-277">[276]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allegations_of_communism">Allegations of communism</span></h3>
<p>For years, Hoover had been suspicious about potential influence of communists in social movements such as labor unions and civil rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[277]</a></sup> Hoover directed the FBI to track King in 1957, and the SCLC as it was established (it did not have a full-time executive director until 1960).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-53">[53]</a></sup> The investigations were largely superficial until 1962, when the FBI learned that one of King's most trusted advisers was New York City lawyer Stanley Levison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKotz2005233_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKotz2005233-279">[278]</a></sup></p>
<p>The FBI feared Levison was working as an "agent of influence" over King, in spite of its own reports in 1963 that Levison had left the Party and was no longer associated in business dealings with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKotz200570.E2.80.9374_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKotz200570.E2.80.9374-280">[279]</a></sup> Another King lieutenant, <a href="/wiki/Hunter_Pitts_O%27Dell" title="Hunter Pitts O'Dell">Hunter Pitts O'Dell</a>, was also linked to the Communist Party by sworn testimony before the <a href="/wiki/House_Un-American_Activities_Committee" title="House Un-American Activities Committee">House Un-American Activities Committee</a> (HUAC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[280]</a></sup> However, by 1976 the FBI had acknowledged that it had not obtained any evidence that King himself or the SCLC were actually involved with any communist organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Church_269-1"><a href="#cite_note-Church-269">[268]</a></sup></p>
<p>For his part, King adamantly denied having any connections to communism, stating in a 1965 <i>Playboy</i> interview that "there are as many Communists in this freedom movement as there are Eskimos in Florida".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWashington1991362_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWashington1991362-282">[281]</a></sup> He argued that Hoover was "following the path of appeasement of political powers in the South" and that his concern for communist infiltration of the Civil Rights Movement was meant to "aid and abet the salacious claims of southern racists and the extreme right-wing elements".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Church_269-2"><a href="#cite_note-Church-269">[268]</a></sup> Hoover did not believe King's pledge of innocence and replied by saying that King was "the most notorious liar in the country".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[282]</a></sup> After King gave his "I Have A Dream" speech during the March on Washington on August 28, 1963, the FBI described King as "the most dangerous and effective Negro leader in the country".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-track_275-1"><a href="#cite_note-track-275">[274]</a></sup> It alleged that he was "knowingly, willingly and regularly cooperating with and taking guidance from communists".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKotz200583_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKotz200583-284">[283]</a></sup></p>
<p>The attempt to prove that King was a communist was related to the feeling of many segregationists that blacks in the South were happy with their lot but had been stirred up by "communists" and "outside agitators".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[284]</a></sup> However, the 1950s and '60s Civil Rights Movement arose from activism within the black community dating back to before World War I. King said that "the Negro revolution is a genuine revolution, born from the same womb that produces all massive social upheavals—the womb of intolerable conditions and unendurable situations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWashington1991363_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWashington1991363-286">[285]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adultery">Adultery</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2392" data-file-width="2183" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg/220px-MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg/330px-MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg/440px-MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
King and <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_X" title="Malcolm X">Malcolm X</a>, March 26, 1964</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Having concluded that King was dangerous due to communist infiltration, the FBI shifted to attempting to discredit King through revelations regarding his private life. FBI surveillance of King, some of it since made public, attempted to demonstrate that he also engaged in numerous extramarital affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-track_275-2"><a href="#cite_note-track-275">[274]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_Johnson" title="Lyndon Johnson">Lyndon Johnson</a> once said that King was a "hypocritical preacher".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[286]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ralph Abernathy stated in his 1989 autobiography <i>And the Walls Came Tumbling Down</i> that King had a "weakness for women", although they "all understood and believed in the biblical prohibition against sex outside of marriage. It was just that he had a particularly difficult time with that temptation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[287]</a></sup> In a later interview, Abernathy said that he only wrote the term "womanizing", that he did not specifically say King had <a href="/wiki/Extramarital_sex" title="Extramarital sex">extramarital sex</a> and that the infidelities King had were <a href="/wiki/Emotional_affair" title="Emotional affair">emotional</a> rather than sexual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abertappva_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-abertappva-289">[288]</a></sup> Abernathy criticized the media for sensationalizing the statements he wrote about King's affairs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abertappva_289-1"><a href="#cite_note-abertappva-289">[288]</a></sup> such as the allegation that he admitted in his book that King had a sexual affair the night before he was assassinated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abertappva_289-2"><a href="#cite_note-abertappva-289">[288]</a></sup> In his original wording, Abernathy had claimed he saw King coming out of his room with a lady when he awoke the next morning and later claimed that "he may have been in there discussing and debating and trying to get her to go along with the movement, I don't know."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abertappva_289-3"><a href="#cite_note-abertappva-289">[288]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his 1986 book <i>Bearing the Cross</i>, David Garrow wrote about a number of extramarital affairs, including one woman King saw almost daily. According to Garrow, "that relationship ... increasingly became the emotional centerpiece of King's life, but it did not eliminate the incidental couplings ... of King's travels." He alleged that King explained his extramarital affairs as "a form of anxiety reduction". Garrow asserted that King's supposed promiscuity caused him "painful and at times overwhelming guilt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[289]</a></sup> King's wife Coretta appeared to have accepted his affairs with equanimity, saying once that "all that other business just doesn't have a place in the very high level relationship we enjoyed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrady200267_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrady200267-291">[290]</a></sup> Shortly after <i>Bearing the Cross</i> was released, civil rights author <a href="/wiki/Howell_Raines" title="Howell Raines">Howell Raines</a> gave the book a positive review but opined that Garrow's allegations about King's sex life were "sensational" and stated that Garrow was "amassing facts rather than analyzing them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[291]</a></sup></p>
<p>The FBI distributed reports regarding such affairs to the executive branch, friendly reporters, potential coalition partners and funding sources of the SCLC, and King's family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[292]</a></sup> The bureau also sent anonymous letters to King threatening to reveal information if he did not cease his civil rights work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[293]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/FBI%E2%80%93King_suicide_letter" title="FBI–King suicide letter">FBI–King suicide letter</a> sent to King just before he received the Nobel Peace Prize read, in part:</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mlk-uncovered-letter.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="626" data-file-width="513" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Mlk-uncovered-letter.png/220px-Mlk-uncovered-letter.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Mlk-uncovered-letter.png/330px-Mlk-uncovered-letter.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Mlk-uncovered-letter.png/440px-Mlk-uncovered-letter.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mlk-uncovered-letter.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/FBI%E2%80%93King_suicide_letter" title="FBI–King suicide letter">FBI–King suicide letter</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suicide_letter_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-suicide_letter-295">[294]</a></sup> mailed anonymously by the FBI</div>
</div>
</div>
<blockquote>
<p>The American public, the church organizations that have been helping—Protestants, Catholics and Jews will know you for what you are—an evil beast. So will others who have backed you. You are done. King, there is only one thing left for you to do. You know what it is. You have just 34 days in which to do (this exact number has been selected for a specific reason, it has definite practical significant  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>]). You are done. There is but one way out for you. You better take it before your filthy fraudulent self is bared to the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKotz2005247_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKotz2005247-296">[295]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>A tape recording of several of King's extramarital liaisons, excerpted from FBI wiretaps, accompanied the letter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrady2002158.E2.80.93159_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrady2002158.E2.80.93159-297">[296]</a></sup> King interpreted this package as an attempt to drive him to suicide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[297]</a></sup> although William Sullivan, head of the Domestic Intelligence Division at the time, argued that it may have only been intended to "convince Dr. King to resign from the SCLC".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Church_269-3"><a href="#cite_note-Church-269">[268]</a></sup> King refused to give in to the FBI's threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-track_275-3"><a href="#cite_note-track-275">[274]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1977, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_District_Judge" title="United States District Judge">Judge</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Lewis_Smith_Jr." title="John Lewis Smith Jr.">John Lewis Smith Jr.</a> ordered all known copies of the recorded audiotapes and written transcripts resulting from the FBI's electronic surveillance of King between 1963 and 1968 to be held in the <a href="/wiki/National_Archives_and_Records_Administration" title="National Archives and Records Administration">National Archives</a> and sealed from public access until 2027.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[298]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Police_observation_during_the_assassination">Police observation during the assassination</span></h3>
<p>A fire station was located across from the Lorraine Motel, next to the boarding house in which James Earl Ray was staying. Police officers were stationed in the fire station to keep King under surveillance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[299]</a></sup> Agents were watching King at the time he was shot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[300]</a></sup> Immediately following the shooting, officers rushed out of the station to the motel. Marrell McCollough, an undercover police officer, was the first person to administer first aid to King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[301]</a></sup> The antagonism between King and the FBI, the lack of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All_points_bulletin" title="All points bulletin">all points bulletin</a> to find the killer, and the police presence nearby led to speculation that the FBI was involved in the assassination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[302]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_recognition">Awards and recognition</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_Jr_with_medallion_NYWTS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3696" data-file-width="2860" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_with_medallion_NYWTS.jpg/220px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_with_medallion_NYWTS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_with_medallion_NYWTS.jpg/330px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_with_medallion_NYWTS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Martin_Luther_King_Jr_with_medallion_NYWTS.jpg/440px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr_with_medallion_NYWTS.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_Jr_with_medallion_NYWTS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
King showing his medallion, which he received from Mayor Wagner</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KellyIngramMLKStatue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="836" data-file-width="400" height="355" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/KellyIngramMLKStatue.jpg/170px-KellyIngramMLKStatue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/KellyIngramMLKStatue.jpg/255px-KellyIngramMLKStatue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/KellyIngramMLKStatue.jpg/340px-KellyIngramMLKStatue.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KellyIngramMLKStatue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of King in Birmingham's <a href="/wiki/Kelly_Ingram_Park" title="Kelly Ingram Park">Kelly Ingram Park</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dexter_Avenue_Baptist.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="631" data-file-width="434" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Dexter_Avenue_Baptist.jpg/170px-Dexter_Avenue_Baptist.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Dexter_Avenue_Baptist.jpg/255px-Dexter_Avenue_Baptist.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Dexter_Avenue_Baptist.jpg/340px-Dexter_Avenue_Baptist.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dexter_Avenue_Baptist.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dexter Avenue Baptist Church, where King ministered, was renamed Dexter Avenue King Memorial Baptist Church in 1978.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>King was awarded at least fifty honorary degrees from colleges and universities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merv_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-merv-304">[303]</a></sup> On October 14, 1964, King became the youngest recipient of the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a>, which was awarded to him for leading nonviolent resistance to racial prejudice in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[304]</a></sup> In 1965, he was awarded the American Liberties Medallion by the <a href="/wiki/American_Jewish_Committee" title="American Jewish Committee">American Jewish Committee</a> for his "exceptional advancement of the principles of human liberty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-merv_304-1"><a href="#cite_note-merv-304">[303]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[305]</a></sup> In his acceptance remarks, King said, "Freedom is one thing. You have it all or you are not free."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[306]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1957, he was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Spingarn_Medal" title="Spingarn Medal">Spingarn Medal</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NAACP" title="NAACP">NAACP</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[307]</a></sup> Two years later, he won the <a href="/wiki/Anisfield-Wolf_Book_Award" title="Anisfield-Wolf Book Award">Anisfield-Wolf Book Award</a> for his book <i>Stride Toward Freedom: The Montgomery Story</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[308]</a></sup> In 1966, the Planned Parenthood Federation of America awarded King the <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Sanger" title="Margaret Sanger">Margaret Sanger</a> Award for "his courageous resistance to bigotry and his lifelong dedication to the advancement of social justice and human dignity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PP_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-PP-310">[309]</a></sup> Also in 1966, King was elected as a fellow of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_AAAS_fellow_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-King_AAAS_fellow-311">[310]</a></sup> In November 1967 he made a 24-hour trip to the United Kingdom to receive an honorary degree from Newcastle University, being the first African-American to be so honoured by Newcastle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[311]</a></sup> In a moving impromptu acceptance speech,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[312]</a></sup> he said</p>
<blockquote>
<p>There are three urgent and indeed great problems that we face not only in the United States of America but all over the world today. That is the problem of racism, the problem of poverty and the problem of war.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1971 he was posthumously awarded a <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Spoken_Word_Album" title="Grammy Award for Best Spoken Word Album">Grammy Award for Best Spoken Word Album</a> for his <i>Why I Oppose the War in Vietnam</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[313]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1977, the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_Medal_of_Freedom" title="Presidential Medal of Freedom">Presidential Medal of Freedom</a> was posthumously awarded to King by President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>. The citation read:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Martin Luther King Jr. was the conscience of his generation. He gazed upon the great wall of segregation and saw that the power of love could bring it down. From the pain and exhaustion of his fight to fulfill the promises of our founding fathers for our humblest citizens, he wrung his eloquent statement of his dream for America. He made our nation stronger because he made it better. His dream sustains us yet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[314]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>King and his wife were also awarded the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Gold_Medal" title="Congressional Gold Medal">Congressional Gold Medal</a> in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[315]</a></sup></p>
<p>King was second in <a href="/wiki/Gallup%27s_List_of_Most_Widely_Admired_People_of_the_20th_Century" title="Gallup's List of Most Widely Admired People of the 20th Century">Gallup's List of Most Widely Admired People of the 20th Century</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[316]</a></sup> In 1963, he was named <a href="/wiki/Time_Person_of_the_Year" title="Time Person of the Year"><i>Time</i> Person of the Year</a>, and in 2000, he was voted sixth in an online "Person of the Century" poll by the same magazine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[317]</a></sup> King placed third in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greatest_American" title="Greatest American">Greatest American</a> contest conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Channel" title="Discovery Channel">Discovery Channel</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/America_Online" title="America Online">AOL</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[318]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials_and_eponymous_places_and_buildings">Memorials and eponymous places and buildings</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MLKStreet.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/MLKStreet.JPG/220px-MLKStreet.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/MLKStreet.JPG/330px-MLKStreet.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/MLKStreet.JPG/440px-MLKStreet.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MLKStreet.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Martin Luther King Jr. Street at <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Bell_Park" title="Liberty Bell Park">Liberty Bell Park</a> in Jerusalem</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Memorial,_San_Francisco.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Memorial%2C_San_Francisco.jpg/220px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Memorial%2C_San_Francisco.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Memorial%2C_San_Francisco.jpg/330px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Memorial%2C_San_Francisco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Memorial%2C_San_Francisco.jpg/440px-Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Memorial%2C_San_Francisco.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Memorial,_San_Francisco.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Martin Luther King Jr. Memorial in <a href="/wiki/Yerba_Buena_Gardens" title="Yerba Buena Gardens">Yerba Buena Gardens</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are numerous memorials to King in the United States, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>More than 730 cities in the United States have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Streets_named_after_Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Streets named after Martin Luther King Jr.">streets named after King</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[319]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/King_County,_Washington" title="King County, Washington">King County, Washington</a>, rededicated its name in his honor in 1986 and changed its logo to an image of his face in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[320]</a></sup></li>
<li>The city government center in <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Harrisburg, Pennsylvania">Harrisburg, Pennsylvania</a>, is named in honor of King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[321]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1980, the U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_the_Interior" title="Department of the Interior">Department of the Interior</a> designated King's boyhood home in Atlanta and several nearby buildings the <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr._National_Historic_Site" title="Martin Luther King Jr. National Historic Site">Martin Luther King Jr. National Historic Site</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp_20110804_323-0"><a href="#cite_note-wp_20110804-323">[322]</a></sup></li>
<li>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr._(Wilson)" title="Martin Luther King Jr. (Wilson)">bust of Dr. King</a> was added to the "gallery of notables" in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a> in 1986, portraying him in a "restful, nonspeaking pose."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artworkd_324-0"><a href="#cite_note-artworkd-324">[323]</a></sup></li>
<li>The beginning words of King's "<a href="/wiki/I_Have_a_Dream" title="I Have a Dream">I Have a Dream</a>" speech are etched on the steps of the <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Memorial" title="Lincoln Memorial">Lincoln Memorial</a>, at the place where King stood during that speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etched_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-etched-325">[324]</a></sup> These words from the speech—"five short lines of text carved into the granite on the steps of the Lincoln Memorial"—were etched in 2003, on the 40th anniversary of the march to Washington, by stone carver Andy Del Gallo, after a law was passed by Congress providing authorization for the inscription.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etched_325-1"><a href="#cite_note-etched-325">[324]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1996, Congress authorized the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Phi_Alpha" title="Alpha Phi Alpha">Alpha Phi Alpha</a> fraternity, of which King is still a member, to establish a foundation to manage fund raising and design of a national <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Memorial" title="Martin Luther King Jr. Memorial">Martin Luther King Jr. Memorial</a> on the <a href="/wiki/National_Mall" title="National Mall">National Mall</a> in Washington, D.C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[325]</a></sup> King was the first African American and the fourth non-president honored with his own memorial in the National Mall area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[326]</a></sup> The memorial opened in August 2011<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[327]</a></sup> and is administered by the <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[328]</a></sup> The address of the monument, 1964 <a href="/wiki/Independence_Avenue_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Independence Avenue (Washington, D.C.)">Independence Avenue, SW</a>, commemorates the year that the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a> became law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[329]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Landmark_for_Peace_Memorial" title="Landmark for Peace Memorial">Landmark for Peace Memorial</a></i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indianapolis,_Indiana" title="Indianapolis, Indiana">Indianapolis, Indiana</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp_20110804_323-1"><a href="#cite_note-wp_20110804-323">[322]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Homage_to_King" title="Homage to King">Homage to King</a></i> sculpture in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlanta,_Georgia" title="Atlanta, Georgia">Atlanta, Georgia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp_20110804_323-2"><a href="#cite_note-wp_20110804-323">[322]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/The_Dream_(sculpture)" title="The Dream (sculpture)"><i>The Dream</i> sculpture</a> in <a href="/wiki/Portland,_Oregon" title="Portland, Oregon">Portland, Oregon</a></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/National_Civil_Rights_Museum" title="National Civil Rights Museum">National Civil Rights Museum</a>, at the Lorraine Motel in <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis, Tennessee</a>, where King died<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp_20110804_323-3"><a href="#cite_note-wp_20110804-323">[322]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brown_Chapel_A.M.E._Church_(Selma,_Alabama)" title="Brown Chapel A.M.E. Church (Selma, Alabama)">Brown Chapel A.M.E. Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Selma,_Alabama" title="Selma, Alabama">Selma, Alabama</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp_20110804_323-4"><a href="#cite_note-wp_20110804-323">[322]</a></sup></li>
<li>On October 11, 2015, the <i>Atlanta Journal-Constitution</i> reported a proposed "Freedom Bell" may be installed atop <a href="/wiki/Stone_Mountain" title="Stone Mountain">Stone Mountain</a> honoring King and his "I Have a Dream" speech, specifically the line "Let freedom ring from Stone Mountain of Georgia."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[330]</a></sup></li>
<li>A <a href="/wiki/Bust_of_Martin_Luther_King_Jr._(Alston)" title="Bust of Martin Luther King Jr. (Alston)">bust of Martin Luther King Jr.</a> has been in the collection of the collection of the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery_(United_States)" title="National Portrait Gallery (United States)">National Portrait Gallery</a> since 1974, and displayed in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> since 2000; a second cast is in the collection of the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_African_American_History_and_Culture" title="National Museum of African American History and Culture">National Museum of African American History and Culture</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>Numerous other memorials honor him around the world, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>The Reverend Martin Luther King Jr. Church in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debrecen,_Hungary" title="Debrecen, Hungary">Debrecen, Hungary</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp_20110804_323-5"><a href="#cite_note-wp_20110804-323">[322]</a></sup></li>
<li>The King-Luthuli Transformation Center in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johannesburg,_South_Africa" title="Johannesburg, South Africa">Johannesburg, South Africa</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp_20110804_323-6"><a href="#cite_note-wp_20110804-323">[322]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Rev. Martin Luther King Jr. Forest in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>'s Southern Galilee region (along with the <a href="/wiki/Coretta_Scott_King" title="Coretta Scott King">Coretta Scott King</a> Forest in Biriya Forest, Israel)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp_20110804_323-7"><a href="#cite_note-wp_20110804-323">[322]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Martin Luther King Jr. School in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accra,_Ghana" title="Accra, Ghana">Accra, Ghana</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wp_20110804_323-8"><a href="#cite_note-wp_20110804-323">[322]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Gandhi-King Plaza (garden), at the India International Center in <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a>, India</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Norfolk,_Virginia" title="Norfolk, Virginia">Norfolk, Virginia</a> stands a memorial in honor of King. The 83-foot-high granite <a href="/wiki/Obelisk" title="Obelisk">obelisk</a> was conceived by former Norfolk Councilman and General District Court Judge Joseph A. Jordan Jr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[331]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[332]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Five_Dollar_Bill">Five Dollar Bill</span></h2>
<p>On April 20, 2016, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treasury_Secretary" title="Treasury Secretary">Treasury Secretary</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacob_Lew" title="Jacob Lew">Jacob Lew</a> announced that the $5, $10, and $20 would all undergo redesign prior to 2020. Lew said that while Lincoln would remain on the obverse of the $5 bill, the reverse would be redesigned to depict various historical events that had occurred at the Lincoln Memorial. Among the planned designs are images from King's "<a href="/wiki/I_Have_a_Dream" title="I Have a Dream">I Have a Dream</a>" speech and the 1939 concert by opera singer <a href="/wiki/Marian_Anderson" title="Marian Anderson">Marian Anderson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[333]</a></sup></p>

HarperCollins. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-06-064691-8" title="Special:BookSources/0-06-064691-8">0-06-064691-8</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMartin+Luther+King+Jr.&amp;rft.aufirst=James+M.&amp;rft.aulast=Washington&amp;rft.btitle=A+Testament+of+Hope%3A+The+Essential+Writings+and+Speeches+of+Martin+Luther+King%2C+Jr&amp;rft.date=1991&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-06-064691-8&amp;rft.pub=HarperCollins&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
